  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_08098bb0-4708-42d4-8269-4ed5b3c82d76'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

      initializeSiwa();

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9457285287319166090"><b>In this extraordinary novel, Karen Maitland delivers a dazzling reinterpretation of Chaucer's <em>Canterbury Tales</em>, an ingenious alchemy of history, mystery, and powerful human drama.</b><br/><br/>The year is 1348. The Black Plague grips the country. In a world ruled by faith and fear, nine desperate strangers, brought together by chance, attempt to outrun the certain death that is running </span>
  <span id="freeText9457285287319166090" style="display:none"><b>In this extraordinary novel, Karen Maitland delivers a dazzling reinterpretation of Chaucer's <em>Canterbury Tales</em>, an ingenious alchemy of history, mystery, and powerful human drama.</b><br /><br />The year is 1348. The Black Plague grips the country. In a world ruled by faith and fear, nine desperate strangers, brought together by chance, attempt to outrun the certain death that is running inexorably toward them.<br /><br />Each member of this motley company has a story to tell. From Camelot, the relic-seller who will become the group's leader, to Cygnus, the one-armed storyteller . . . from the strange, silent child called Narigorm to a painter and his pregnant wife, each has a secret. None is what they seem. And one among them conceals the darkest secret of all—propelling these liars to a destiny they never saw coming.<br /><br />Magical, heart-quickening, and raw, <em>Company of Liars</em> is a work of vaulting imagination from a powerful new voice in historical fiction.<br /><br />(Length: 17 hours, 40 minutes)</span>
  <a data-text-id="9457285287319166090" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    I think it depicts a world where fantasy &amp; reality isn't as distinct, all the stories were equally plausable &amp; implausable. But I also think they made<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    I think it depicts a world where fantasy &amp; reality isn't as distinct, all the stories were equally plausable &amp; implausable. But I also think they made it clear when they said "I'm going to tell a story now" you were to take that as fiction.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>

<div id="review_780264077" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/780264077" />
    <a title="Jaidee " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17295672-jaidee"><img alt="Jaidee " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558439086p2/17295672.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/780264077?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaidee " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaidee " href="/user/show/17295672-jaidee">Jaidee </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11316827-company-of-liars">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those on spiritual quests</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jaidee  by: </span>
      a very wise young woman who shall remain nameless
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17295672-jaidee?shelf=five-stars-books">five-stars-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer780264077" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7317085311510176142">5 "fascinating, provocative, emotional " stars !!<br/><br/><b> 7th Favorite Read of 2017 (tie) </b><br/><br/> This is a wonderful debut novel that I have been wanting to read for a few years and finally gotten to. I was immersed from beginning to end and was such an interesting combination of genres !<br/><br/> Was this an adult fairy tale, fable, fantasy, historical fiction or what ? It was all of the above and so skillfully done that it made complete and utter sense as I was reading.<br/><br/> The author was able to tell a very dark tale i</span>
  <span id="freeText7317085311510176142" style="display:none">5 "fascinating, provocative, emotional " stars !!<br /><br /><b> 7th Favorite Read of 2017 (tie) </b><br /><br /> This is a wonderful debut novel that I have been wanting to read for a few years and finally gotten to. I was immersed from beginning to end and was such an interesting combination of genres !<br /><br /> Was this an adult fairy tale, fable, fantasy, historical fiction or what ? It was all of the above and so skillfully done that it made complete and utter sense as I was reading.<br /><br /> The author was able to tell a very dark tale in the most entertaining of ways and I wanted to steal time from chores, work and even conversation from my partner in order to continue to read this.<br /><br /> I was warned to not read the last page by my GR buddy Hanneke but of course I did not listen and in this instance it was ok (often in my case it is not) as the ending worked very well for me but I can understand how it would not work for others.<br /><br /> This book takes place during the 14th century in England where a motley group of travellers come together and wander the countryside in order to escape the plague. However, the metaphorical conditon they are trying to escape is shame. Some of them survive, others do not. As I am writing this I realize what a skilled and wise author this would need to be in order to do it in an illuminating, fascinating and entertaining wayl<br /><br /> I will definitely be reading more from this author as this book was AWESOME !!<br /><br /> I want to leave you with a quote and I think an important antidote to the internalized shame that many of us carry :<br /><br /> <i> It has taken me months, years perhaps , to fathom the answer. Home is the place you return to when you have finally lost your soul. Home is the place where life is born, not the place of your birth, but the place where you seek rebirth. When you no longer remember which tale of your own past is true and which is an invention, when you know that "you" are an invention, this is the time to seek out your home. Perhaps only when you have come to understand that can you finally reach home </i><br /><br /> Sit and ponder that my lovelies !!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7317085311510176142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2282953711" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2282953711" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63168028-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516912354p2/63168028.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2282953711?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/63168028-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=top-10">top-10</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2282953711" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16592506641923129366"><b>Deception</b><br/>At the onset of the black plague in 1348, a group of travellers band together to seek refuge against the disease and journey northwards to a safe location. Each member of the group has a hidden secret, and one, in particular, has something more sinister to conceal. The secrets they each reveal are shocking and illustrate the type of characters they are and the backgrounds they each come from. Karen has a wonderful ability to capture not only the characterisation but the interaction and </span>
  <span id="freeText16592506641923129366" style="display:none"><b>Deception</b><br />At the onset of the black plague in 1348, a group of travellers band together to seek refuge against the disease and journey northwards to a safe location. Each member of the group has a hidden secret, and one, in particular, has something more sinister to conceal. The secrets they each reveal are shocking and illustrate the type of characters they are and the backgrounds they each come from. Karen has a wonderful ability to capture not only the characterisation but the interaction and underlying motives within the group. As each secret is revealed one by one the group start dying. Must the killer be amongst them or are they being hunted? The final secret is a surprise and keeps you guessing right to the end.<br /><br />The plot is engrossing and holds you in a spell, the characters are superb, and the pace is perfect. The sense of time and daily living conditions are vividly brought to life. The wonderful writing creates such magnificent images of landscape, hardships and a constant atmosphere of foreboding. Karen has this wonderful ability to characterise the landscape and give it its own mesmerising role in the story.<br /><br />The Black Plague between 1348 and 1350 would kill over 1.5 million people, over one-third of the population of England. Amongst this great plague was set a supernatural and religious supposition. One graphic testimony can be found at St Mary's, Ashwell, Hertfordshire, where an anonymous hand has carved a harrowing inscription for the year 1349: <i> "Wretched, terrible, destructive year, the remnants of the people alone remain."</i><br /><br />It’s just a pity that a novel like this comes to an end, and the only thing left to do is seek out another Karen Maitland book. She is definitely the queen of the medieval historical thriller. This book is well and truly rooted in my favourites shelf and Karen is one of my favourite authors.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16592506641923129366" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16743647247118840058" style="display:none">Enjoyed this historical novel! A mystery tale of a band of travelers in The Middle Ages, travelling through England to escape the upcoming pestilence.... <br />Entertaining read! The ending unexpected, at least for me.... Will definitely try more of this author. <br />As usual... probably more to follow. For those who like historical fiction, recommended!<br /><br />And for those who don't know this book yet (many have read it here I see): <br />Midsummer's day 1348... On this day of ill omen, plague makes its entrance. Within weeks, swathes of Engeland will be darkened by death's shadow as towns and villages burn to the ringing of church bells. While panick and suspicion flood the land, a small band of travelers comes together to outrun the pestilence, travelling through the ravaged countryside: amongst others a scarred trader in holy relics, a conjuror, two musicians, and a deformed storyteller.. . But when one of their number is found hanging from a tree, the chilling discovery confirms that something more sinister than plague is in their midst. And as the runes warn of treachery, it appears no one is quite what they seem, least of all the child rune reader, who mercilessly compels each of her companions to tell their stories and face the consequences...</span>

<div id="review_2620461369" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2620461369" />
    <a title="Beverly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68708752-beverly"><img alt="Beverly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505852430p2/68708752.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2620461369?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beverly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beverly" href="/user/show/68708752-beverly">Beverly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2620461369&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2620461369" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12711636975738197779">Oh dear, I am so glad I wasn't born during the Middle Ages. Karen Maitland makes you feel like you are there, with all the superstition, filth, disease and hypocrisy of the church that defined the time. The poor suffer mightily. The company that we find ourselves with are traveling merchants of a sort, the homeless and wretched who have come together for safety against the weather, the pestilence, (plague has broken out) and vicious robbers that roam the land.<br/><br/>This is no Canterbury Tales with hum</span>
  <span id="freeText12711636975738197779" style="display:none">Oh dear, I am so glad I wasn't born during the Middle Ages. Karen Maitland makes you feel like you are there, with all the superstition, filth, disease and hypocrisy of the church that defined the time. The poor suffer mightily. The company that we find ourselves with are traveling merchants of a sort, the homeless and wretched who have come together for safety against the weather, the pestilence, (plague has broken out) and vicious robbers that roam the land.<br /><br />This is no Canterbury Tales with humour along with the suffering; there is nothing funny about these travelers. They are all hiding from their past, and the lies they tell are to protect themselves from society, the church or the law. If there is one criticism I would level at the book it is that after the author has made you scoff at the superstitious nonsense they believe in, she ends the tale with the supernatural.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12711636975738197779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2521148527" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2521148527" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2521148527?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2521148527" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10388123968713867621"><b>The Holy Relic Swindler’s Tale</b><br/><br/>It is I suppose comforting for some to believe that the social issues of today’s Britain are perennial, that there is a national character, perhaps, which continuously muddles through the same problems over and over. This is one explanation for Karen Maitland’s imagined world of England in the Middle Ages. The way she portrays the state of the nation - from immigration to the condition of the roads; from sexual harassment to fake news - suggests that the problems we</span>
  <span id="freeText10388123968713867621" style="display:none"><b>The Holy Relic Swindler’s Tale</b><br /><br />It is I suppose comforting for some to believe that the social issues of today’s Britain are perennial, that there is a national character, perhaps, which continuously muddles through the same problems over and over. This is one explanation for Karen Maitland’s imagined world of England in the Middle Ages. The way she portrays the state of the nation - from immigration to the condition of the roads; from sexual harassment to fake news - suggests that the problems we have to deal with have a constancy that define the country.<br /><br />I further suppose that without such presumed continuity, there wouldn’t be much of a market for her type of historical fiction. In order for a story set in the 14th century to be comprehensible there has to be something more than geography which connects us culturally to that distant era. So Maitland projects our fears and anxieties into the past, not unlike much of sci-fi projects them into the future. Among other things, in the latter such a literary tactic allows for some familiarity about the problems as well as creativity in imagining their solutions or their ultimate consequences.<br /><br />But there is a clear difference between historical fiction and sci-fi. We already have (and are) the solutions to the problems of the past. So the genre of historical fiction can only work if it can suggest how we arrived where we are. If there’s not much sociological variation from where we started, the setting of the story is quaint but largely irrelevant, and, from a literary perspective, fraudulent, an unintentional parody. Why not set the tale in Ancient Rome? Or Victorian England? Or contemporary New York City? The allusions to things like xenophobia, commercial fraud, knife crime, child and substance abuse, and the English Summer weather could be made where and whenever. ’Plus ça change, plus c’est la meme chose’ would seem sufficient to get the point across. <br /><br />Casting faux historical references in terms of a sort of hippie Canterbury Tale (with neither the wit nor the elegance of the original) is, therefore, of dubious merit. Dropping in archaic period terms like ‘camelot’ and ‘kirtle’ don’t do much to divert attention from the Hobbesian misery of the lives of the characters - solitary, nasty, brutish and short. These characters inhabit a land of superstitious squalor in which the principle recreations are alcoholism and GBH. Whatever secrets they might be hiding seem insignificant in light of their existential reality, which has little to do with their place in history.<br /><br />It could be that I’m being unfair. Perhaps the <i>Company of Liars </i>is an allegory about the 21st century rather than a projection to the 14th. Could it be that we can only recognize the extent of our depravity by considering it in terms of some distant condition? If so, The book might have some merit. Otherwise it is a tedious journey to nowhere. Agatha Christie’s <i>Ten Little Indians</i>, to name just one parallel story, is a far superior alternative - established firmly in a time and place with no pretensions to period color, and consequently much more honest..</span>
  <a data-text-id="10388123968713867621" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_75792263" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75792263" />
    <a title="Hilary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/229103-hilary"><img alt="Hilary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1186075886p2/229103.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75792263?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hilary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hilary" href="/user/show/229103-hilary">Hilary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75792263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5046905150774357072">I've read multiple novels about the Plague (Year of Wonders, Doomsday Book, probably others I can't think of right now). This was a completely different experience. The Plague is, in some ways, huge, and in other ways a minor character. A nod to the Canterbury Tales, this is the story of a motley group of travelers thrown together in their desperate attempt to escape the ravages of the pestilence.<br/><br/>I found this to be a total immersion experience. It doesn't wallow in medieval language, but the voc</span>
  <span id="freeText5046905150774357072" style="display:none">I've read multiple novels about the Plague (Year of Wonders, Doomsday Book, probably others I can't think of right now). This was a completely different experience. The Plague is, in some ways, huge, and in other ways a minor character. A nod to the Canterbury Tales, this is the story of a motley group of travelers thrown together in their desperate attempt to escape the ravages of the pestilence.<br /><br />I found this to be a total immersion experience. It doesn't wallow in medieval language, but the vocabulary and ideas are faithful to the period. The characters have great depth as people, but also effectively showcase some controversial issues (without the narrator or the author taking a modern stance).<br /><br />One of the things I liked best about Company of Liars is its lack of hysteria. The travelers are understandably afraid of the Plague, and of the many other dangers of their time, and many terrible things happen along the way (honestly, this isn't a spoiler, what would you expect of a Plague novel anyway?), but the storytelling is honest and totally lacking in melodrama.<br /><br />Although I initially balked at the first person narration, it turned out to be completely appropriate and much more emotionally compelling than third person would have been.<br /><br />While it had a slow start, by the time I was a few chapters in I was completely hooked and burned through this at top speed. Highly recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5046905150774357072" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10999621439962535822" style="display:none">Karen Maitland is officially my current favourite author. Company of Liars is set in 1348 with the plague spreading throughout England, causing chaos and fear. It's everyone for themselves. The story is told through the unreliable narrator Camalot. As the band of misfit travellers trudge through the country to find sanctuary and safety, each harbour a personal secret and are prepared to do anything to conceal it. The story is peppered with aspects of fairytales, myths and legends, making the travellers' journey chillingly atmospheric. Maitland also explores medieval thought that merges religious and superstitious belief for the old ways. Her extensive research is spot-on as it explores the different perceptions, understanding and presentation of truth through a historical lens.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=zck">zck</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2215903177" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1599912668185933059">Set in 1348 England, nine strangers form a group and travel together to escape the spreading pestilence. The narrator, Camelot, is a seller of religious relics. He is joined by a magician, a musician, the musician’s young apprentice, a storyteller, a fortune-teller, a midwife, and an artist and his wife. The fortune teller, a peculiar child, reads runes, and she foretells of impending malevolent events. The travelers tell tales around the nighttime fire, revealing parts of the past, though not a</span>
  <span id="freeText1599912668185933059" style="display:none">Set in 1348 England, nine strangers form a group and travel together to escape the spreading pestilence. The narrator, Camelot, is a seller of religious relics. He is joined by a magician, a musician, the musician’s young apprentice, a storyteller, a fortune-teller, a midwife, and an artist and his wife. The fortune teller, a peculiar child, reads runes, and she foretells of impending malevolent events. The travelers tell tales around the nighttime fire, revealing parts of the past, though not always the truth. Gradually, their secrets are discovered. <br /><br />Superstitions of the era influence the journey. They believe in witches and the supernatural. They think a wolf or werewolf is following them. Dramatic tension is maintained by wondering what evils will befall the group. The narrative is driven by the sequential uncovering of secrets. The tone is eerie. The reader will feel a sense of foreboding. We know something bad is going to happen and are waiting for the axe to fall. <br /><br />I do not usually enjoy scary stories, but I quite enjoyed this one. It is well-crafted. I was drawn into the storyline of each character and interested in their journey. It reminds me of a darker version of <i>The Canterbury Tales,</i> sans Middle English verse, which I am sure is intentional on the author’s part. <br /></span>

<div id="review_708549639" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/708549639" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6087599-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530478892p2/6087599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/708549639?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/6087599-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer708549639" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17424608364086346668"><br/>Okay, let's start with the negative, just to get it out of the way. I had read several reviews on Goodreads complaining about the ending before I even picked up the book, so all through the last chapter I kept thinking <i>Whew! That's not such a bad ending! I can see how it might not please everyone, but I actually kind of like it! </i> That was quite a relief, considering I adored the book most of the way through, and it had already gone down a bit in my estimation by the final chapter. My relief last</span>
  <span id="freeText17424608364086346668" style="display:none"><br />Okay, let's start with the negative, just to get it out of the way. I had read several reviews on Goodreads complaining about the ending before I even picked up the book, so all through the last chapter I kept thinking <i>Whew! That's not such a bad ending! I can see how it might not please everyone, but I actually kind of like it! </i> That was quite a relief, considering I adored the book most of the way through, and it had already gone down a bit in my estimation by the final chapter. My relief lasted until the final page and then <i>BAM</i>, I suddenly knew exactly what those reviewers were talking about. So yes, add me to the ranks of disconsolate souls bemoaning the ending. It was formulaic and rather ugly, with a cliched side of "leave 'em hangin'!" and just, <i>UGH.</i> Such a let down. I found myself desperately trying to make the ending work in my head, so it wouldn't spoil the book for me. And there is at least one interpretation that "fixes" it, if Camelot can be viewed as an unreliable narrator. Which... I think one can make a fair argument for. It was after all a very superstitious era, and Camelot clearly believes many of the folk beliefs and tales. The aspect of the plot which seems to actually contain supernatural elements could be a fantasy/skewed reality he creates, as he, along with the rest of Europe, struggles to grasp the horrible reality of plague and the violence/tragedy it inspires. If viewed as manifestations of Camelot's troubled psychosis (and perhaps as metaphorical instead of literal) those aspects of the book I disliked actually add power to the story. So, I shall choose to go with that interpretation. :) (Just humor me, okay???)<br /><br />ANYWAY, the ending was not enough to ruin this dark, Medieval fairytale for me. I love well written historical fiction complete with gritty historical detail and strong characters, and this book is a splendid example. Maitland has a brilliant grasp of Medieval British history, and I loved the way she wove in all the superstition and belief in/terror of the supernatural that plagued people in that era (especially as they struggled to understand and "prevent" plague). The characters are for the most part wonderfully engaging- I grew deeply attached to many of them (sucks for me ) and wanted to jump inside the book to throttle others, so I definitely think Maitland excels there. Overall, I loved it!<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17424608364086346668" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1443759721" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1443759721" />
    <a title="Srividya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27599745-srividya"><img alt="Srividya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499431918p2/27599745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1443759721?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Srividya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Srividya" href="/user/show/27599745-srividya">Srividya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/17798184-company-of-liars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=world-lit-2016">world-lit-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=2016-reading-challenge">2016-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=on-the-edge-of-your-seat">on-the-edge-of-your-seat</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=celebrate-1000">celebrate-1000</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=kindle-owned">kindle-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=england">england</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=medieval">medieval</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1443759721" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer386323072699744182"><b>1.5 stars rounded to 2</b><br/><br/><b>
  <i>“The most important things are the hardest to say. They are the things you get ashamed of, because words diminish them -- words shrink things that seemed limitless when they were in your head to no more than living size when they're brought out. But it's more than that, isn't it? The most important things lie too close to wherever your secret heart is buried, like landmarks to a treasure your enemies would love to steal away. And you may make revelations that cost you de</i></b></span>
  <span id="freeText386323072699744182" style="display:none"><b>1.5 stars rounded to 2</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>“The most important things are the hardest to say. They are the things you get ashamed of, because words diminish them -- words shrink things that seemed limitless when they were in your head to no more than living size when they're brought out. But it's more than that, isn't it? The most important things lie too close to wherever your secret heart is buried, like landmarks to a treasure your enemies would love to steal away. And you may make revelations that cost you dearly only to have people look at you in a funny way, not understanding what you've said at all, or why you thought it was so important that you almost cried while you were saying it. That's the worst, I think. When the secret stays locked within not for want of a teller but for want of an understanding ear.” <br />Stephen King</i>
</b><br /><br />Secrets are a part and parcel of our lives and to hide them we often take the help of lies to ensure that these secrets stay hidden and protected. This is almost everyone’s story and I honestly feel that each person is entitled to their secrets and lies. What man does not lie? It could be those small white lies said to make others happy or even the big ones, told majorly to ensure some secrets are kept safe and no damage occurs to their lives and the lives of their loved ones? Can these people honestly be blamed for those lies that are often disguised, maybe badly, as ways to help keep their loved ones safe? Can people be blamed for keeping secrets that when revealed would lead to their persecution? Human nature is tuned in such a way that it will do anything, absolutely anything, to escape their own persecution or the persecution of those they love and therefore lying in such a context doesn’t seem like a major sin to them. Or maybe they feel that they are justified in telling such lies and don’t fear the consequences that they will face because of it. As in the quote above ‘a secret stays a secret until it finds an understanding ear’. <br /><br />So also with these nine people who form the main core group of this group, a company of liars as they are called, secrets are abound and lies are told aplenty. The difference being, they are persecuted because of the lies that they have told or the secrets that they have kept. Karen Maitland’s book deals with the onslaught of persecution of liars in the backdrop of the plague. The fact that each of them dies is something that is given in the blurb. How and why they die is what this book is all about. An extremely interesting premise, which when combined with the fact that it is set in the medieval times and is also called ‘a story of the plague’, made me extremely excited to read it. And read it, I did, in fact, finished the whole book in a single day, which is quite a thing as I haven’t done something like that in ages. However, I was disappointed, completely disappointed with the whole thing. The only thing which made me give it a 1.5 star rating rounded off to 2 was the fact that I loved the way the whole group came together and some of their story telling was fantastical in nature. It had a lilt of poetry and fairy tale in some places, which kind of kept my interest going together with the fact that I wanted to know what happened at the end. <br /><br />Let’s first talk about what I liked in this book. The book had a fascinating array of characters, ranging from a Camelot to a magician to musicians to a healer and a rune teller and some others. Each of them had a hidden secret and each of them were lying about themselves. Moreover, it was set in the backdrop of the plague (oh this is a grouse as well, which I shall dwell upon later) that beset England during the medieval times. And that’s about it – that’s exactly what I loved about it, the premise. It truly went downhill from there and never recovered. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>“Perhaps that is where our choice lies -- in determining how we will meet the inevitable end of things, and how we will greet each new beginning.” <br />- Elana K. Arnold, Burning</i>
</b><br /><br />Isn’t this a fact of life? With such a motley cast, you would expect the author to create a magnum opus but unfortunately Ms. Maitland disappoints. Her characters, though with a lot of depth thanks to their secrets, are never developed beyond the fact that they reveal their true selves and then die. There is no learning curve, there is no development curve and worse there is no depth to these stories, which at times feel like they were put there just to give these characters something to say or some back history for the sake of it. While some of their stories are almost akin to fairy tales and are quite interesting, some fall flat, especially that of the magician. While the entire book is recited from the point of view of the Camelot, there is much that could have been done but hasn’t been. In fact, I felt the lack of different POVs acutely in this book. A book where there are many different characters should ideally have different POVs, which would have made it more interesting and definitely more vibrant. However, it’s lack makes the book flat and quite monotonous and often repetitive in nature. <br /><br />Now to my second and perhaps the biggest grouse about this book. On the cover it is written that this is and I quote ‘a novel of the plague’. However, the plague and the medievalism forms the backdrop of the novel and not the main course. It could have been titled ‘a novel in the backdrop of the plague’ and it would have been more accurate. For the plague forms a reason for these people to band together and doesn’t affect them in the least. Of course, the author does talk about the plague raging England at that time and its effect on the populace but it is in passing. It is more to create a path for these people to move along than anything else. Neither are they shown fighting it nor do the actual people form a part of this story. Why then make it a novel of the plague? Honestly, I don’t know.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>“The plague of mankind is the fear and rejection of diversity: monotheism, monarchy, monogamy and, in our age, monomedicine. The belief that there is only one right way to live, only one right way to regulate religious, political, sexual, medical affairs is the root cause of the greatest threat to man: members of his own species, bent on ensuring his salvation, security, and sanity.”<br />Thomas Szasz</i>
</b><br /><br />Being a novel set in the medieval times, it is often difficult not to touch upon the politics and religion and their nexus. However, here again I was disappointed. Don’t get me wrong, Ms. Maitland does cover all these topics and more but it is again merely in passing than with any depth. We do get involved with the exigencies put by the Church on the people, the narrowness of thought during that time, the fear of the Church in the minds of the people, the sheer callousness of the bishops and the priests but what was lacking was depth. It felt like reading a news item where we learn about facts but not the thought process. In a book that is set in the medieval times and a book that is quite lengthy in its prose, I felt that there should have been more information or description rather than mere actions. The facts are so obvious that even a person not well versed in the history of those times will be able to tell you all about them. What makes a book different is how it handles those facts and how it portrays the period. Here again Ms. Maitland disappoints with her pure factual narration that is synced with the story line, making the writing dry and quite unimpressive.<br /><br />Not all is bad in this book, for the suspense factor is maintained throughout. By this I don’t mean that we don’t know the murderer, in fact we do. However, the why of those murders and why does this person murder the others is kept a suspense throughout. Unfortunately though, it remains a mystery even at the end. No reason, no conclusion, nothing is provided by the author and it is left to the reader to fathom. While I don’t usually mind such narratives, this one irked me a lot. It felt as if the author took us readers for a ride, promising a lot of things but not delivering it. I felt completely let down and felt that I had actually wasted a lot of time reading this book. It was the end that kept me going through all the boring and repetitive parts, it was the hope that there would be some clarifications, but I got naught. In fact, at that point, I would have settled for a fantastical if implausible ending but I didn’t even get that. What I got was a stereotyped ending, which did nothing to endear me to this book.<br /><br />I would end by saying that this book had a lot going for it but it was not followed upon. Most of the twists were something that I had already figured out, so it held no mystery for me. Neither was the writing so great that I could write reams on it. All in all, a disappointment for me. I wouldn’t recommend it to anyone but if you feel like taking a dip into these waters, please do so. Who knows, you might end up liking it, as tastes always differ.<br /><br />PS - My initial rating was 2.5 rounded off to 3 but while writing this review I felt that I had more grouses than likes, which is why I changed it to 1.5 rounded off to 2. </span>
  <a data-text-id="386323072699744182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1292963304" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1292963304" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1292963304?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=2019-aty-challenge">2019-aty-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=kindle-purchase">kindle-purchase</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=death">death</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=medieval">medieval</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1292963304" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11540710382331378699"><b>
  <i>Strange how desperate people are to know the future, even if they know they cannot change it.<br/><br/>Besides, if you can read the future, you can read the past for they are but ends of the same thread, and I always take great care that no one should know anything of me except my present.</i>
</b><br/><br/>A Company of Liars is the story of a group of nine strangers who travel together in an effort to escape the plague that is spreading death throughout England. It is not simply a story about travelers or pestilence, h</span>
  <span id="freeText11540710382331378699" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>Strange how desperate people are to know the future, even if they know they cannot change it.<br /><br />Besides, if you can read the future, you can read the past for they are but ends of the same thread, and I always take great care that no one should know anything of me except my present.</i>
</b><br /><br />A Company of Liars is the story of a group of nine strangers who travel together in an effort to escape the plague that is spreading death throughout England. It is not simply a story about travelers or pestilence, however, it is also a story about darkness, superstition, secrets and guilt. Each of the travelers has a secret, and it is the interpersonal relationships and the unraveling of those secrets that keep the reader enthralled.<br /><br />There are a few historical inaccuracies that spring off the page in a somewhat jarring way, and there are sensibilities expressed that would seem unlikely, if not impossible, in the 14th Century setting, but those are not enough to undo the good parts of this novel. The narrator, Camelot, is well-drawn and one easily slides into seeing the events through his eyes. It doesn’t take much to guess who the true culprit among the travelers is, but that does not spoil the fun either, because you want to know why more than who and you certainly want to know each person’s story and how they came to this place in their lives.<br /><br />For me to give a book a 4 or 5-star rating, it almost always has to offer something deeper than just a good tale. This is a good tale, but I think there is also a layer beneath that which explores the purpose of lies in the lives of men and whether that withholding of the truth can ever be a good thing. What tortures these people are not really outside forces but the things they know about themselves--the things they lie about. It begs the question of whether all of us are hiding something, and whether we lie to ourselves about why we lie. So, 4-stars for leaving me with something to ponder when I go to my rest tonight. Excellent debut effort.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11540710382331378699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/317032637?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=queer">queer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=historical-fiction-alternatehistory">historical-fiction-alternatehistory</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer317032637" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10559256616153613309">I picked up Company of Liars as my fifth book of the readathon, last night, and read half of it in one go. Unfortunately, I couldn't stay up, but I can definitely say this for it: it could distract me from the pain of gallstones when high doses of anti-spasmodics and opiates could not. I think how much it entertains you will depend on how much you buy into the characters: I was prepared to fall for most of them, and to pity those I didn't adore, so I got swept up in their story. It's a relativel</span>
  <span id="freeText10559256616153613309" style="display:none">I picked up Company of Liars as my fifth book of the readathon, last night, and read half of it in one go. Unfortunately, I couldn't stay up, but I can definitely say this for it: it could distract me from the pain of gallstones when high doses of anti-spasmodics and opiates could not. I think how much it entertains you will depend on how much you buy into the characters: I was prepared to fall for most of them, and to pity those I didn't adore, so I got swept up in their story. It's a relatively slow-paced story, I suppose: the actual threat doesn't come into the open until almost the end. <br /><br />There are clues throughout as to what is going to happen, not just what will happen next, but what will unfold throughout the rest of the story. Some of the hints are fairly large; most readers will probably guess ahead of the plot, but it was the pleasure of fitting everything <i>together</i> that kept my interest -- this and that I already knew, but what significance it could have...<br /><br />There are criticisms in other reviews about the range of characters and how well they took each other's secrets: there's little shock and outrage at a character who is gay or characters who commit incest. I felt... it is a little anachronistic, but it also worked for me because all the characters have secrets they dare not reveal, and all of them have weaknesses laid bare to the others in their group. They need each other, and can't afford to have the group fall apart.<br /><br />I can understand those who found it too slow paced, and those who felt the clues were too obvious. I was a little exasperated by the anagram: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Narigorm = Morrigan. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> That felt <i>clumsy</i>. Still, I bought into the characters and I badly wanted them to do well, and I bought into untangling the mystery. I enjoyed it a lot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10559256616153613309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1515645061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1515645061" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51395417-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506113266p2/51395417.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1515645061?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/51395417-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385341691" href="/book/show/3184477-company-of-liars">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1515645061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4222626909823753870">On the surface this novel is a medieval suspense thriller. When one reads this text on the critical level it becomes a haunting allegory on the nature of death, truth, hope, religion, and love. You can read this book on the literal level and enjoy it. (Think a better written Dan Brown novel, except Ms. Maitland knows how to end a book.) However, to read this text on the surface level is to shortchange the reader. If one looks for the obvious symbolism and philosophical context, this text will st</span>
  <span id="freeText4222626909823753870" style="display:none">On the surface this novel is a medieval suspense thriller. When one reads this text on the critical level it becomes a haunting allegory on the nature of death, truth, hope, religion, and love. You can read this book on the literal level and enjoy it. (Think a better written Dan Brown novel, except Ms. Maitland knows how to end a book.) However, to read this text on the surface level is to shortchange the reader. If one looks for the obvious symbolism and philosophical context, this text will stick with you long after you turn the final page.<br />The novel follows nine travelers running from the "Black Death" in 1348 England. The hard nature of life in those times is clearly drawn by Maitland and sticks with the reader. Hunger and death are accepted parts of everyday life. These characters don't complain, they deal. The difference to how we act today is staggering.<br />Through her wonderfully drawn characters Maitland explores the power of lies and how we use them to construct our reality. The text can lead its readers to endless debates about the power and need for lies in all of our lives.<br />Although the villain of the piece was obvious to me, the ambiguity of what the villain represents is still not secure in my mind. I don't think Maitland wants it to be. Is the villain a symbol for death, for the destructiveness of lies, or of holy judgment? All are perfectly possible.<br />(The preceding paragraph makes much more sense once you have read the book. I don't want to spoil anything!)<br />The narrator of this text (Camelot) is a wonderful voice to carry us through the proceedings and creates a sympathetic prism from which to view the other characters, all of whom have unlikable traits, but most of whom I was able to feel some empathy with.<br />The only drawbacks to the text are some small historical errors, and the fact that occasionally some of the characters seem to have 21st century values in 14th century England. These few moments jar the reader out of the world of the novel, and this detracts from the text. The character of Rodrigo is the character who most suffers from Maitland inserting her thinly veiled views on homosexuality and the Christian Church into the text. This hurts the novel.<br />Still, I greatly enjoyed this text, and I did not see the ending coming. Yet, the ending really does work, even if at first it seems outlandish.<br />The past does indeed creep up on us all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4222626909823753870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer15263052250807340496">This is a LLLLLOOOONG book. It took me ages to read it. But there was never a time when my interest plopped or when the base 9 characters who make the "company" didn't leave me wanting more. Each and every one of those 9 was so finely framed and every line of their emotions, core desires, acting out or vocal habits - all those aspects that are covered by the term "personality"- they were THERE. Karen Maitland has a gift for giving us their onus and their spirit.<br/><br/>This is a book I could react to or</span>
  <span id="freeText15263052250807340496" style="display:none">This is a LLLLLOOOONG book. It took me ages to read it. But there was never a time when my interest plopped or when the base 9 characters who make the "company" didn't leave me wanting more. Each and every one of those 9 was so finely framed and every line of their emotions, core desires, acting out or vocal habits - all those aspects that are covered by the term "personality"- they were THERE. Karen Maitland has a gift for giving us their onus and their spirit.<br /><br />This is a book I could react to or describe for 50 paragraphs. But I will not.<br /><br />Suffice to say that the plague sets the tone. The villages and the English countryside are stench real. And the outcomes, despite the level of the tales told by the company, never seemed to be one of any hope or optimism. Not by this reader. <br /><br />The white haired mite? From the very first I was repelled. Child NOT!<br /><br />Anyway, it's tales in a tale. It has some good guys. It has tons of mutilation. Human and animal. It is tooth and claw survival. But it is also the gifts of human mind in the worst of times which hold nothing else but horrendous bleakness and suffering.<br /><br />Gird your loins for this one though. It is LONG! <br /><br />I almost subtracted another star for the ideology that Maitland sneaks within some of her chapter headings and paragraph length quotes. Oh, they are definitely quotable. But I will put none of them here. Because some of them are more than incorrect to my life's opinion, and they simply don't come from that plague century either.<br /><br />Lastly, I have to add this. The ending disappointed me. It was rather a cop out or else she is going to do a sequel. I'm sure the second is a good guess.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12724518225640928306">There's not a better way to end the year of great reads in 2008 than to end it with Karen Maitland's first book, Company of Liars. This book has everything: love, death, friendship, witchcraft, deception...it's a little historical fiction mixed with a little fantasy rolled in to one yummy nugget of a novel.<br/><br/>The plot was excellent, the storytelling was just amazing and the characters are ones you are not soon to forget.   <span id="freeText12724518225640928306" style="display:none">There's not a better way to end the year of great reads in 2008 than to end it with Karen Maitland's first book, Company of Liars. This book has everything: love, death, friendship, witchcraft, deception...it's a little historical fiction mixed with a little fantasy rolled in to one yummy nugget of a novel.<br /><br />The plot was excellent, the storytelling was just amazing and the characters are ones you are not soon to forget. This is one of those that stay with you a while. I find myself missing Camelot the most.<br /><br />My favorite quote: "Home is the place you return to when you have finally lost your soul. Home is the place where life is born, not the place of your birth, but the place where you seek rebirth". - Camelot<br /><br />I recommend Company of Liars to anyone who appreciates good storytelling.<br /><br />Amy Says: 5 / 5<br /><br />Karen Maitland's new book, The Owl-Killers is due out in the UK on March 26, 2009.</span>

<div id="review_206491416" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/206491416" />
    <a title="Orsolya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5647191-orsolya"><img alt="Orsolya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603139114p2/5647191.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/206491416?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orsolya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orsolya" href="/user/show/5647191-orsolya">Orsolya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385341691" href="/book/show/3184477-company-of-liars">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=medieval-times">medieval-times</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer206491416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11717953113779268084">Imagine yourself on a cross-country road trip picking up hitch-hikers at various stops. This adventure would involve danger, suspense, fear, and maybe even growth (emotionally). Adapt this to the 14th century with wanderers on foot attempting to escape the pestilence and you have Karen Maitland’s “Company of Liars”. <br/><br/>Maitland’s novel is a juicy concoction of a medieval historical fiction novel mixed with spiritualism, magic, and mystery dumped into a pot of a fairy tale/fable core served to adult</span>
  <span id="freeText11717953113779268084" style="display:none">Imagine yourself on a cross-country road trip picking up hitch-hikers at various stops. This adventure would involve danger, suspense, fear, and maybe even growth (emotionally). Adapt this to the 14th century with wanderers on foot attempting to escape the pestilence and you have Karen Maitland’s “Company of Liars”. <br /><br />Maitland’s novel is a juicy concoction of a medieval historical fiction novel mixed with spiritualism, magic, and mystery dumped into a pot of a fairy tale/fable core served to adults versus the Disney crowd (fear not: it is not a fantasy novel and more on the historical fiction end). “Company of Liars” transports the reader to a dark, rainy, medieval forest on foot with the many characters of the story despite if the sun is shining outside your own window. One can almost hear the thunder clap. “Company of Liars” has a simple plot (various travelers come together by twists of fate passing through England); and yet the novel is gripping and quite entertaining with strong visuals and emotional threads. <br /><br />“Company of Liars” features a variety of characters (Camelot, Zophiel, Rodrigo, Jofre, Pleasance, Narigorm, Osmond, Adela, etc); each with his/her own personality and quirks. The characters feel real and accessible both in a medieval and modern sense. Maitland retains a level of history but with a readable prose and interpretation.<br /><br />Undeniably, “Company of Liars” has a fairy tale foundation with recognizable elements (trolls, witches, wolves) but on an adult level (for example: robbers living under bridges that collect tolls --- clear a troll). Again though, don’t expect a fantasy novel as “Company of Liars” is certainly not that; but there are subtle hints of childhood tales from the dark side. <br /><br />Despite some of these familiar undertones; Maitland’s text is not predictable or overly foreshadowed. Therein lays a healthy amount of drama with the need to know what happens next. However, there are some much too dramatized and unbelievable moments. These aren’t too excessive but when they are apparent, they sadly take a bit away from the story.<br /><br />A major issue with “Company of Liars” is the unnatural ease with which the characters accept each other and their mysteries. Yes, there are arguments and disagreements but overall, the loyalties of the strangers to each other are a bit difficult to digest. On the other hand, this adds to the foggy, mysterious allure of “Company of Liars”. <br /><br />Although minor, there are some errors with the text such as a character leaving the scene and then is said to have spoken but it was clearly meant to be another character. I am not sure if that made sense, but the point is that there are some errors which the editor appears to have missed. <br /><br />Maitland’s strength of presenting the facts of medieval life in a subtle manner is quite evident in the novel. Readers learn about the time period in a natural and amusing way. Maitland obviously conducted research for “Company of Liars” instead of taking a pure fictional route. <br /><br />Unfortunately, “Company of Liars” becomes tedious as the story progresses with the plot being repetitive. This <u>is</u> the premise of the tale and therefore must be accepted but readers searching for a more detailed and multidimensional narrative may find themselves to be disappointed. <br /><br />The final chapters of “Company of Liars” are weak and a bit too far-fetched like a bad horror movie. This picks up again in the last chapter which reverts back to the fairy tale form and dives into the depths of the plot’s morals. However, this then again nose dives – big time—with the concluding lines which will literally have the reader let out a groan accompanied by a, “Come one!” to say the least. Basically, the ending is a let down and takes away from the novel drastically. <br /><br />Although the conclusion may be unsavory; Maitland’s after word describing the historical merits and liberties of her novel plus a glossary of terms is well-received and notable. <br /><br />“Company of Liars” is a unique fairy tale-esque novel which will satisfy both HF and medieval mystery readers (except for perhaps the ending). Although Maitland’s piece isn’t perfect, the story is compelling and certainly sweeps the reader away as all good books should do. “Company of Liars” is strongly recommended and much enjoyed. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11717953113779268084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2585464558" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2585464558" />
    <a title="Miglė" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76439836-migl"><img alt="Miglė" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515916235p2/76439836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2585464558?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miglė" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miglė" href="/user/show/76439836-migl">Miglė</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76439836-migl?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76439836-migl?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2585464558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11463363661985666480">It's an engaging book about 9 travellers in the backdrop of the plague (we don't see much of the plague here, though). All of them have secrets and tensions, and there are murders and a bit of mystical stuff.<br/>To me it didn't have much 'historical' wibe, I saw this narrative as some kind of RPG in a Medieval setting with a hint of "mafia" (you know, the party game). When you look at it this way, it is actually very compelling and well written and the ending might not bother you that much. <br/><br/>The auth</span>
  <span id="freeText11463363661985666480" style="display:none">It's an engaging book about 9 travellers in the backdrop of the plague (we don't see much of the plague here, though). All of them have secrets and tensions, and there are murders and a bit of mystical stuff.<br />To me it didn't have much 'historical' wibe, I saw this narrative as some kind of RPG in a Medieval setting with a hint of "mafia" (you know, the party game). When you look at it this way, it is actually very compelling and well written and the ending might not bother you that much. <br /><br />The author knows Middle Ages well, there are references to real places and customs, also, her settings are usually shabby and gritty, which adds to the atmosphere. <br /><br />I have to say that I liked the book in general, so sorry in advance for the thoughts about historical fiction that keep bothering me from time to time. I wanted to get them in the writing, and even thought the critique is partly inspired by this book, I mean it more in general.<br /><br />So I have no idea what people in Middle ages were like. Sure, they weren't completely alien to us. But there must have been some profound differences in the worldview, right? <br />And the characters in this book felt just like XXI century people - and probably that's one of the reasons they seemed so likeable: you can identify with them!<br /><br />I'm not saying all Medieval people should be depicted as absolute brutes, but in this book there is one particular character (out of 9) who is openly sexist, homophobic and antisemitic, and he is the one depicted as a terribly bad person in general, while most of the others seem pretty open-minded even by today's standards. Sure, we can imagine that there's a very specific set of characters, not representative of the whole population, but then, I don't know, maybe they could meet some of the population? I mean, the majority of my parents' generation are (sadly) pretty sexist and homophobic, but they're not all mustache-twirling, throat-slashing movie villains! For God's sake, my own grandmother thinks that red-haired children are born evil and she is literate and watches TV!<br /><br />Sorry for the rant, this is probably not about this book, but about historical fiction in general. We want characters to be like us in some ways and to have similar values, but then the mindset of the period gets a bit confused. <br /><br />But again - if you think about it as a Middle-ages inspired fantasy book, or, as I said "RPG+Mafia" kind of book, it's great and open-minded modern characters don't seem out of place.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11463363661985666480" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6698931145781809498" style="display:none">Camelot decides to travel with two musicians, a magician, a painter and his wife, a swan-boy storyteller, and a soothsayer as they travel away from the pestilence a.k.a. "Black Plague" as it ravages and destroys everything in its path. Something evil is following them too. As it begins to kill them off one by one, can they make it to their destination safely even though they are a company of liars? Read on and find out for yourself.<br /><br />This was a pretty good historical horror mystery novel. I also liked it how it was a unique version of the Canterbury tales too. If you like these types of stories that are set in the dark ages, definitely check it out for yourself. This is available at your local library and wherever books are sold. </span>
